<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41586"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Oil palm genome sequence reveals divergence of interfertile species in Old and New worlds"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature 2013 500:7462"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2013-07-24"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2013 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="The genome sequence of the African oil palm, the main source of oil production, is used to predict at least 34,802 genes, including oil biosynthesis genes; comparison with the draft sequence of the South American oil palm reveals that the two species may have diverged in the New World and that segmental duplications of chromosome arms define the palaeotetraploid origin of palm trees. Two papers published in this issue of Nature deal with the genetics of two variants of one of the most important crops in use today &#8212; the African oil palm Elaeis guineensis and its South American cousin Elaeis oleifera. Palm oil accounts for almost half the edible oil consumed worldwide and is also a biofuel, although not without controversy, as in many areas palm oil monoculture has replaced valuable natural forest. Analyses of the 1.8-gigabase genome sequence of E. guineensis and draft sequence of E. oleifera provide insights into oil biosynthesis genes and their regulators, and a record of genome evolution. A key event in the domestication and breeding of the oil palm was loss of the thick, coconut-like shell. The second of the two papers identifies mutations the SHELL gene that specify the different fruit forms found in the oil palm and shows that SHELL gene mutations that originated in pre-colonial Africa are responsible for the single gene hybrid vigour and high yields attained by the oil palm. Oil palm is the most productive oil-bearing crop. Although it is planted on only 5% of the total world vegetable oil acreage, palm oil accounts for 33% of vegetable oil and 45% of edible oil worldwide, but increased cultivation competes with dwindling rainforest reserves. We report the 1.8-gigabase (Gb) genome sequence of the African oil palm Elaeis guineensis, the predominant source of worldwide oil production. A total of 1.535&#8201;Gb of assembled sequence and transcriptome data from 30&#8201;tissue types were used to predict at least 34,802 genes, including oil biosynthesis genes and homologues of WRINKLED1 (WRI1), and other transcriptional regulators1, which are highly expressed in the kernel. We also report the draft sequence of the South American oil palm Elaeis oleifera, which has the same number of chromosomes (2n = 32) and produces fertile interspecific hybrids with E. guineensis2 but seems to have diverged in the New World. Segmental duplications of chromosome arms define the palaeotetraploid origin of palm trees. The oil palm sequence enables the discovery of genes for important traits as well as somaclonal epigenetic alterations that restrict the use of clones in commercial plantings3, and should therefore help to achieve sustainability for biofuels and edible oils, reducing the rainforest footprint of this tropical plantation crop."/>

        <meta property="og:description" content="The genome sequence of the African oil palm, the main source of oil production, is used to predict at least 34,802 genes, including oil biosynthesis genes; comparison with the draft sequence of the South American oil palm reveals that the two species may have diverged in the New World and that segmental duplications of chromosome arms define the palaeotetraploid origin of palm trees."/>

itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Michael-Hogan" data-author-popup="auth-Michael-Hogan">Michael Hogan</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff2">2</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization"             <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs3" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs3-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs3">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs3-content"><p>Oil palm is the most productive oil-bearing crop. Although it is planted on only 5% of the total world vegetable oil acreage, palm oil accounts for 33% of vegetable oil and 45% of edible oil worldwide, but increased cultivation competes with dwindling rainforest reserves. We report the 1.8-gigabase (Gb) genome sequence of the African oil palm <i>Elaeis guineensis</i>, the predominant source of worldwide oil production. A total of 1.535 Gb of assembled sequence and transcriptome data from 30 tissue types were used to predict at least 34,802 genes, including oil biosynthesis genes and homologues of <i>WRINKLED1</i> (<i>WRI1</i>), and other transcriptional regulators<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Bourgis, F. et al. Comparative transcriptome and metabolite analysis of oil palm and date palm mesocarp that differ dramatically in carbon partitioning. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 12527–12532 (2011)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d21969e770">1</a></sup>, which are highly expressed in the kernel. We also report the draft sequence of the South American oil palm <i>Elaeis oleifera,</i> which has the same number of chromosomes (2<i>n</i> = 32) and produces fertile interspecific hybrids with <i>E. guineensis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Hardon, J. J. &amp; Tan, G. Y. Interspecific hybrids in the genus Elaeis I. crossability, cytogenetics and fertility of F1 hybrids of E.  guineensis × E.  oleifera. Euphytica 18, 372–380 (1969)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d21969e783">2</a></sup> but seems to have diverged in the New World. Segmental duplications of chromosome arms define the palaeotetraploid origin of palm trees. The oil palm sequence enables the discovery of genes for important traits as well as somaclonal epigenetic alterations that restrict the use of clones in commercial plantings<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Jaligot, E. et al. Epigenetic imbalance and the floral developmental abnormality of the in vitro-regenerated oil palm Elaeis guineensis. Ann. Bot. 108, 1453–1462 (2011)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d21969e787">3</a></sup>, and should therefore help to achieve sustainability for biofuels and edible oils, reducing the rainforest footprint of this tropical plantation crop.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The genus <i>Elaeis</i> (tribe <i>Cocoseae</i>) is in the family <i>Arecaceae</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Dransfield, J. et al. Genera Palmarum: The Evolution and Classification of Palms (Royal Botanic Gardens Kew, 2008)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d21969e831">4</a></sup>, one of the oldest families of flowering plants, with fossils dating from the Cretaceous period<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Purseglove, J. W. In Tropical Crops (Monocotyledons) 416–510 (Longman, 1972)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d21969e835">5</a></sup>. The genus consists of two species, <i>E. guineensis</i> from West Africa<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Zeven, A. C. The origin of the oil palm. J. Niger. Inst.Oil Palm Res. 4, 218–225 (1965)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d21969e843">6</a></sup> and <i>E. oleifera</i> from Central and South America. <i>E. guineensis</i> has a higher yield, but <i>E. oleifera</i> has higher unsaturated fatty acid content, lower height, and resistance to disease<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Cochard, B., Amblard, P. &amp; Durand-Gasselin, T. Oil palm genetic improvement and sustainable development. Oleagineux Corps Gras Lipides 12, 141–147 (2005)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d21969e856">7</a></sup>. Commercial cultivation of oil palm commenced on the West African coast in the early twentieth century<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Hartley, C. The Oil Palm 47–94 (Longman, 1988)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d21969e860">8</a></sup>. In southeast Asia, where it is one of the most important commercial crops, the first recorded oil palm was brought from Africa through Mauritius and Amsterdam in 1848 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Corley, R. H. V. &amp; Tinker, P. B. In The Oil Palm 4th edn, 1–26 (Blackwell Science, 2003)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d21969e864">9</a>), when four seedlings were planted as ornamentals in the Bogor Botanical Gardens in Java. Commercial cultivation began in the early twentieth century and despite the long breeding cycle (10 to 12 years) and large land requirement for field trials<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Mayes, S., Jack, P. L., Corley, R. H. &amp; Marshall, D. F. Construction of a RFLP genetic linkage map for oil palm (Elaeis guineensis Jacq.). Genome 40, 116–122 (1997)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d21969e868">10</a></sup>, high yield breeding materials (up to 12 tonnes per hectare per year (t ha<sup>−1</sup> yr<sup>−1</sup>) (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Corley, R. H. V. &amp; Tinker, P. B. In The Oil Palm 4th edn, 1–26 (Blackwell Science, 2003)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d21969e875">9</a>)) have been developed in less than 100 years. As such, the largely undomesticated oil palm is an ideal candidate for genomic-based tools including expressed sequence tags (ESTs)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Jouannic, S. et al. Analysis of expressed sequence tags from oil palm (Elaeis guineensis). FEBS Lett. 579, 2709–2714 (2005)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d21969e879">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Ho, C. –L. et al. Analysis and functional annotation of expressed sequence tags (ESTs) from multiple tissues of oil palm (Elaeis guineensis Jacq.). BMC Genomics 8, 381 (2007)." href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d21969e882">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Low, E. T. L. et al. Oil palm (Elaeis guineensis Jacq.) tissue culture ESTs: identifying genes associated with callogenesis and embryogenesis. BMC Plant Biol. 8, 62 (2008)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d21969e885">13</a></sup> and transcriptome sequencing of the oil palm fruit during development, maturation and ripening<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Bourgis, F. et al. Comparative transcriptome and metabolite analysis of oil palm and date palm mesocarp that differ dramatically in carbon partitioning. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 12527–12532 (2011)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d21969e890">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Tranbarger, T. J. et al. Regulatory mechanisms underlying oil palm fruit mesocarp maturation, ripening, and functional specialization in lipid and carotenoid metabolism. Plant Physiol. 156, 564–584 (2011)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d21969e893">14</a></sup> to harness the potential of this remarkably productive crop.</p><p>We sequenced the approximately 1.8-Gb <i>E. guineensis</i> genome (AVROS (Algemene Vereniging van Rubberplanter ter Oostkust van Sumatra) <i>pisifera</i> fruit form) to high coverage with a combination of Roche/454 GS FLX Titanium (Roche/454) and Sanger bacterial artificial chromosome (BAC) end sequencing (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#MOESM16">Supplementary Figs 1–3</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#MOESM16">Supplementary Tables 1–5</a> and Methods). The combined total length of the assembly (a fifth genome build or P5-build) is 1.535 Gb. Comparison of the P5-build to genetic linkage maps (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#MOESM16">Supplementary Fig. 4</a> and Methods) resulted in 16 genetic scaffolds (one per chromosome; 43% of the P5 scaffold assembly) for a final total of 40,072 scaffolds with an N50 (the scaffold size above which 50% of the total length of the sequence assembly can be found) of 1.27 megabases (Mb) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#MOESM16">Supplementary Table 4</a>) and 74% of RefSeq supported genes. For comparison, the genome of <i>E. oleifera</i> was also sequenced with a combination of fragment and linker libraries (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#MOESM16">Supplementary Table 2</a> and Methods). Thirty transcriptome libraries were sequenced and assembled, producing 4,528–18,936 isotigs (unique transcript assemblies) per library (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#MOESM16">Supplementary Fig. 5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#MOESM16">Supplementary Table 6</a>). We sequenced 298,039 reads from methylation-filtered genomic libraries from Deli <i>dura</i> and <i>pisifera</i> genotypes of <i>E. guineensis</i> and <i>E. oleifera</i> (Methods). Methylation-filtered libraries included 90% of the gene models and were enriched 5.6 times for genes with a ‘gene space’ of between 300 and 400 Mb, comparable to that of rice and maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Palmer, L. E. et al. Maize genome sequencing by methylation filtration. Science 302, 2115–2117 (2003)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d21969e944">15</a></sup>.</p><p>The guanine–cytosine content of the <i>E. guineensis</i> genome (37%) is similar to that of other plant genomes, including the date palm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Al-Dous, E. K. et al. De novo genome sequencing and comparative genomics of date palm (Phoenix dactylifera). Nature Biotechnol. 29, 521–527 (2011)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d21969e954">16</a></sup>, but genes were conspicuous for having a much higher guanine–cytosine content (50%). Gene-finding algorithms (Methods) predicted 158,946 gene candidates covering 92 Mb of exonic gene space (5% of the 1.8-Gb genome sequence) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#MOESM16">Supplementary Tables 4 and 5</a>). Of these candidates, 34,802 were similar to known proteins at the peptide sequence level with 96% observed in transcriptome data (Methods). Of the remaining 124,144 candidates, 15,311 were identified in transcriptome data (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#MOESM16">Supplementary Table 4</a>). Known retroelements (67,169) and other transposons (41,664) made up the remaining 108,833 candidates. Comparison to all repetitive element classes resulted in the identification of 775,703 independent genomic regions matching repetitive sequence elements, corresponding to 282 Mb of sequence (or 18% of the P5-build), with 39% guanine–cytosine content (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#MOESM16">Supplementary Table 4</a>). Repeat content of the unmapped and unassembled contigs was far higher, as expected, and estimated to be approximately half of the 1.53-Gb P5-build, or 57% of the 1.8-Gb <i>E. guineensis</i> genome.</p><p>The 16 EG5 chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>) were numbered according to size and compared with previous mapping (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#MOESM16">Supplementary Table 7</a>) and fluorescence <i>in situ</i> hybridization studies (FISH)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Castilho, A. M., Vershinin, A. V. &amp; Heslop-Harrison, J. S. Repetitive DNA and the chromosomes in the genome of oil palm (Elaeis guineensis). Ann. Bot. 85, 837–844 (2000)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d21969e983">17</a></sup>. Gene density (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>) was distributed unevenly: five of the smallest six chromosomes had one gene-rich arm, and one repeat-rich arm, as shown previously with FISH<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Castilho, A. M., Vershinin, A. V. &amp; Heslop-Harrison, J. S. Repetitive DNA and the chromosomes in the genome of oil palm (Elaeis guineensis). Ann. Bot. 85, 837–844 (2000)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d21969e991">17</a></sup>. Known repeat classes matching the TIGR grass repeat database and REPBASE were distributed in gene-poor, methylated regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#Fig1">Fig. 1b, c</a>), whereas simple di- and trinucleotide repeats<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Castilho, A. M., Vershinin, A. V. &amp; Heslop-Harrison, J. S. Repetitive DNA and the chromosomes in the genome of oil palm (Elaeis guineensis). Ann. Bot. 85, 837–844 (2000)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d21969e998">17</a></sup> were mostly within genic regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#Fig1">Fig. 1d</a>). Potential centromeric regions were identified using an internally repetitive pericentromeric repeat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Castilho, A. M., Vershinin, A. V. &amp; Heslop-Harrison, J. S. Repetitive DNA and the chromosomes in the genome of oil palm (Elaeis guineensis). Ann. Bot. 85, 837–844 (2000)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1005">17</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#Fig1">Fig. 1e</a>, purple), whereas highly conserved TTTAGGG telomeric repeat arrays were identified at the extreme ends of 7 of the 32 chromosome arms (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#Fig1">Fig. 1e</a>, green). The most prominent 5S ribosomal RNA cluster (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#Fig1">Fig. 1e</a>, orange) was found on the largest chromosome, whereas the only telocentric chromosome was one of the two smallest, as described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Castilho, A. M., Vershinin, A. V. &amp; Heslop-Harrison, J. S. Repetitive DNA and the chromosomes in the genome of oil palm (Elaeis guineensis). Ann. Bot. 85, 837–844 (2000)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1019">17</a></sup>. Two interstitial telomere repeat arrays on chromosomes 2 and 14 were embedded within putative centromeric regions. Robertsonian fusions of telocentric chromosome ends may have given rise to these two chromosomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Richards, E. J., Chao, S., Vongs, A. &amp; Yang, J. Characterization of Arabidopsis thaliana telomeres isolated in yeast. Nucleic Acids Res. 20, 4039–4046 (1992)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1023">18</a></sup>, and date palm has 18 chromosomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Al-Dous, E. K. et al. De novo genome sequencing and comparative genomics of date palm (Phoenix dactylifera). Nature Biotechnol. 29, 521–527 (2011)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1027">16</a></sup>, consistent with this hypothesis. Typical of monocot genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Du, J. et al. Evolutionary conservation, diversity and specificity of LTR-retrotransposons in flowering plants: insights from genome-wide analysis and multi-specific comparison. Plant J. 63, 584–598 (2010)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1031">19</a></sup>, the most abundant repetitive elements were <i>copia</i> (33%) and <i>gypsy</i> (8%) retroelements, as well as other long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons (6%) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#MOESM16">Supplementary Fig. 6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#MOESM16">Supplementary Methods</a>). Interestingly, 47% of all repeats observed were uncharacterized previously, with 73% absent from <i>E. oleifera</i> and 99% absent from <i>Musa acuminata</i> (banana). The distribution of repeats in methylation-filtered reads indicated that RIRE1 and other <i>copia</i> elements are especially heavily methylated.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="The chromosomes of oil palm."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: The chromosomes of oil palm.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature12309/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature12309/MediaObjects/41586_2013_Article_BFnature12309_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature12309/MediaObjects/41586_2013_Article_BFnature12309_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="684" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><i>E. guineensis</i> has 16 chromosome pairs, ordered by size, which correspond to 16 linkage groups identified by genetic mapping (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#MOESM16">Supplementary Table 7</a>). Tracks displayed are: <b>a</b>, gene density; <b>b</b>, methyl-filtered read density; <b>c</b>, retroelement density; <b>d</b>, simple sequence repeats; <b>e</b>, low copy number repetitive elements, including telomere repeat TTTAGGG (green), 5S rRNA (orange) and pericentromeric repeats (purple); <b>f</b>, regional G-C content (range 0.3–0.45); <b>g</b>, genetically mapped scaffolds from the P5-build; and <b>h</b>, segmental duplications. Densities for telomere repeats are exaggerated for visual clarity.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#MOESM12">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature12309/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Comparison of <i>E. guineensis</i> chromosomes to each other revealed that oriented homologous duplicated sequences (segmental duplications) are notably abundant (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#Fig1">Fig. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#MOESM16">Supplementary Fig. 7</a>). Analysis of conserved gene order revealed that the duplications were retained in <i>E. oleifera</i>, so that segmental duplications pre-dated the divergence of the African and South American oil palm (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#MOESM16">Supplementary Fig. 8a</a>). Given that most of the genome is represented by segmental duplications, and not triplications, we conclude that oil palm is a palaeotetraploid, in line with speculation based on cytogenetics and restriction fragment length polymorphism (RFLP) mapping<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Castilho, A. M., Vershinin, A. V. &amp; Heslop-Harrison, J. S. Repetitive DNA and the chromosomes in the genome of oil palm (Elaeis guineensis). Ann. Bot. 85, 837–844 (2000)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1134">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Singh, R. et al. Identification of cDNA RFLP markers and their use for molecular mapping in oil palm. Asia Pac. J. Mol. Biol. Biotechnol. 16, 53–63 (2008)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1137">20</a></sup>. These duplications do not span the putative pericentromeric regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>), indicating that most centromeres arose after polyploidization, consistent with extensive chromosome restructuring. Homologues of 94.4%, 83.5% and 80.2% of the genes from <i>Phoenix dactylifera, M. acuminata</i> and <i>Arabidopsis</i> respectively, were found in <i>E. guineensis</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#MOESM16">Supplementary Table 8</a> and Methods). Each <i>E. guineensis</i> duplication matched unique scaffolds in the date palm genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#MOESM16">Supplementary Fig. 8b</a>), indicating that date palms have most of the segmental duplications found in oil palm. Polyploidization has been inferred by chromosome counts in only a limited number of <i>Arecaceae</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Wood, T. E. et al. The frequency of polyploid speciation in vascular plants. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 106, 13875–13879 (2009)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1168">21</a></sup>, and a likely scenario is that the progenitor of both palms arose as a polyploid. We performed a similar analysis of the banana genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="D'Hont, A. et al. The banana (Musa acuminata) genome and the evolution of monocotyledonous plants. Nature 488, 213–217 (2012)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1172">22</a></sup> and found extensive synteny between each oil palm chromosome and several chromosomes from banana (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#MOESM16">Supplementary Fig. 9</a>), confirming that duplication events in the <i>M. acuminata</i> genome occurred after the <i>Musa–Elaeis</i> split, as proposed previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="D'Hont, A. et al. The banana (Musa acuminata) genome and the evolution of monocotyledonous plants. Nature 488, 213–217 (2012)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1186">22</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Gene model comparisons."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Gene model comparisons.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature12309/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature12309/MediaObjects/41586_2013_Article_BFnature12309_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature12309/MediaObjects/41586_2013_Article_BFnature12309_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="403" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Venn Diagram illustrating the proportion of shared gene family clusters in <i>E. guineensis</i>, <i>M. acuminata</i>, <i>A.</i> <i>thaliana</i> and <i>P. dactylifera</i>. Number of genes (clusters) compared were: <i>A.</i> <i>thaliana,</i> 27,416 (15,399); <i>M. acuminata</i>, 36,529 (16,874); <i>P. dactylifera</i>, 28,882 (15,979) and <i>E. guineensis</i>, 34,802 (16,374). <b>b</b>, Gene ontology classifications of oil palm and date palm. <b>c</b>, Ratio of gene number (oil palm:date palm) in each gene ontology classification. Deg, degradation; FA, fatty acid; hexoses-P, hexose phosphate pathway; mal and pyr met, malate and pyruvate metabolism; mt, mitochondrial; OPP, oxidative pentose phosphate; pt PLP syn, plastidial phospholipid synthesis; TAG, triacylglycerol; TPs, transporters.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#MOESM13">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature12309/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The 34,802 sequence similarity gene predictions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#MOESM16">Supplementary Table 4</a>) were annotated for gene ontology terms (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#Fig2">Fig. 2b, c</a>), with a focus on oil biosynthesis (Methods). Oil synthesis in the kernel commences at 11 to 12 weeks after anthesis (WAA) and is complete by 15 to 16 WAA at which stage mesocarp oil synthesis starts, reaching a peak at 20 WAA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Sambanthamurthi, R., Sundram, K. &amp; Tan, Y. Chemistry and biochemistry of palm oil. Prog. Lipid Res. 39, 507–558 (2000)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1265">23</a></sup>. In plants, <i>de novo</i> fatty acid synthesis (FAS) is compartmentalized in plastids, whereas triacylglycerol (TAG) synthesis occurs in the cytoplasm. Although the oil palm accumulates markedly higher TAG than the date palm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Bourgis, F. et al. Comparative transcriptome and metabolite analysis of oil palm and date palm mesocarp that differ dramatically in carbon partitioning. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 12527–12532 (2011)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1272">1</a></sup>, the number of genes involved in TAG biosynthesis is notably similar in both palms. In contrast, FAS genes have higher representation in the oil palm genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>). Apart from the transcriptomes of 30 tissues (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#Fig3">Fig. 3a, b</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#MOESM16">Supplementary Fig. 5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#MOESM16">Supplementary Table 6</a>), in-depth sequencing of kernel and mesocarp (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#MOESM16">Supplementary Table 9</a>) indicated that the transcriptome signatures are similar in both tissues with plastidial FAS genes upregulated compared to TAG. Thus, the enzymes of the Kennedy pathway for TAG assembly must cope with the high flux of <i>de novo</i> fatty acids in oil palm. FAS genes were upregulated markedly just before the onset, and then declined during the peak of lipid accumulation. This contrasts with previous reports that FAS transcripts continue to increase in the mesocarp<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Bourgis, F. et al. Comparative transcriptome and metabolite analysis of oil palm and date palm mesocarp that differ dramatically in carbon partitioning. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 12527–12532 (2011)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1299">1</a></sup>. This may reflect the <i>tenera</i> fruit form used in this study, as <i>dura</i> has a longer maturation period (22 WAA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Bafor, M. E. &amp; Osagie, A. U. Changes in lipid class and fatty acid composition during maturation of mesocarp of oil palm (Elaeis guineensis) variety dura. J. Sci. Food Agric. 37, 825–832 (1986)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1309">24</a></sup> than <i>tenera</i> (20 WAA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Sambanthamurthi, R., Sundram, K. &amp; Tan, Y. Chemistry and biochemistry of palm oil. Prog. Lipid Res. 39, 507–558 (2000)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1317">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Shaarani, S. M., Cárdenas-Blanco, A., Amin, M. H. G., Soon, N. G. &amp; Hall, L. D. Monitoring development and ripeness of oil palm fruit (Elaeis guneensis) by MRI and bulk NMR. Int. J. Agric. Biol. 12, 101–105 (2010)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1320">25</a></sup>. WRI1 regulates oil accumulation in the oil palm mesocarp<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Bourgis, F. et al. Comparative transcriptome and metabolite analysis of oil palm and date palm mesocarp that differ dramatically in carbon partitioning. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 12527–12532 (2011)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1324">1</a></sup> and we found highest mesocarp expression of WRI1 just before lipid accumulation onset. However, kernel showed 75-fold higher WRI1 expression compared to mesocarp (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#MOESM16">Supplementary Table 9</a>), implying its pivotal role in kernel oil synthesis. <i>LEAFY COTYLEDON1</i> (<i>LEC1</i>), <i>LEC2, ABSCISIC ACID INSENSITIVE3</i> (<i>ABI3</i>) and <i>FUSCA3</i>, which activate <i>WRI1</i> in oilseeds, were not found in mesocarp transcriptomes, but were well-represented in the kernel, with <i>LEC1</i> and <i>ABI3</i> showing higher expression at the start and completion of oil accumulation, respectively. Interestingly, the transcriptional regulator <i>PICKLE</i> (<i>PKL</i>) was expressed in both the mesocarp and kernel (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#MOESM16">Supplementary Table 9</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Lipid and carbohydrate metabolism in oil palm fruits."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Lipid and carbohydrate metabolism in oil palm fruits.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature12309/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature12309/MediaObjects/41586_2013_Article_BFnature12309_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature12309/MediaObjects/41586_2013_Article_BFnature12309_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="783" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b>, <b>b</b>, Number of lipid-synthesis-related (<b>a</b>) and carbohydrate-synthesis-related (<b>b</b>) gene transcripts in different tissues. <b>c</b>, Comparison of gene-expression levels between kernel and mesocarp tissue before and at peak of oil accumulation.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#MOESM14">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature12309/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Genes involved in sucrose degradation and the oxidative pentose phosphate pathway were more highly represented in oil palm than date palm (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>). Pentose phosphates are recycled into glucose 6-phosphate to fuel glycolytic pathways, and import of these cytosolic metabolites requires specific transporters on the plastid envelope. Although these transporter genes are upregulated strongly in oil palm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Bourgis, F. et al. Comparative transcriptome and metabolite analysis of oil palm and date palm mesocarp that differ dramatically in carbon partitioning. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 12527–12532 (2011)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1415">1</a></sup>, they are similarly represented in date and oil palm genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>). Thus, channelling of sugars destined for oil synthesis is regulated at the transcriptome level in oil palm. Additional insights important to TAG biosynthesis, fruit ripening and abscission are provided in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#MOESM16">Supplementary Notes</a>.</p><p>To place palms on the evolutionary tree, evidence-based gene models from each species were combined with a previous seed plant data set<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Lee, E. K. et al. A functional phylogenomic view of the seed plants. PLoS Genet. 7, e1002411 (2011)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1428">26</a></sup> to form a matrix of 1,685 gene partitions (858,954 patterns) and 107 taxa. <i>P. dactylifera</i>, <i>E. guineensis</i> and <i>E. oleifera</i> are present in 1,206, 1,229 and 1,190 partitions, respectively. All three were well separated from other monocots (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#Fig4">Fig. 4</a>), including nearest neighbours <i>Musa</i> (banana), <i>Curcuma</i> (turmeric) and <i>Zingiber</i> (ginger). Phylogenetic dating using conservative constraints (Methods) predicted a divergence 65 million years (Myr) ago between date and oil palm, and 51 Myr between <i>E. oleifera</i> and <i>E. guineensis</i>. This is comparable with divergence between Old and New world relatives such as African <i>Sorghum bicolor</i> (sorghum) and American <i>Zea mays</i> (maize) panicoid grasses (26 Myr). Unlike maize and sorghum, however, <i>E. guineensis</i> and <i>E. oleifera</i> give rise to fertile hybrids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Hardon, J. J. &amp; Tan, G. Y. Interspecific hybrids in the genus Elaeis I. crossability, cytogenetics and fertility of F1 hybrids of E.  guineensis × E.  oleifera. Euphytica 18, 372–380 (1969)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1473">2</a></sup>, consistent with the vicariant hypothesis for phylogeographical divergence, in which geographically isolated species are under no selective pressure to evolve reproductive isolation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Riggins, C. W. &amp; Seigler, D. S. The genus Artemisia (Asteraceae: Anthemideae) at a continental crossroads: molecular insights into migrations, disjunctions, and reticulations among Old and New World species from a Beringian perspective. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 64, 471–490 (2012)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1477">27</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Phylogenetic analysis."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Phylogenetic analysis.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature12309/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature12309/MediaObjects/41586_2013_Article_BFnature12309_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature12309/MediaObjects/41586_2013_Article_BFnature12309_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="560" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>A carefully annotated subset of proteins from <i>E. guineensis</i>, <i>E. oleifera</i> and <i>P. dactylifera</i> were included in a matrix of 1,685 gene partitions (858,954 patterns) and 107 taxa. This matrix is extracted from partitions with at least 30 taxa present in a much larger matrix. A maximum likelihood tree of monocotyledonous taxa is shown, along with bootstrap values when less than 100 (Methods). Scale bar indicates the mean number of substitutions per site.</p><p>
                        <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#MOESM15">PowerPoint slide</a>
                      </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature12309/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The genome sequence of oil palm will be a rich resource for oil palm breeders, geneticists and evolutionary biologists alike. It has revealed that palms are ancient tetraploids, and that the African and South American species probably diverged in the Old and New Worlds. Over-represented genes in lipid and carbohydrate metabolism are expressed differentially in mesocarp and kernel, accounting for the different properties of palm fruit and palm kernel oils. The genome sequence will also enable mapping of somaclonal epigenetic alterations that restrict the use of clones in commercial plantings. The dense representation of sequenced scaffolds on the genetic map will facilitate identification of genes responsible for important yield and quality traits. The genome sequence of this tropical plantation crop is an important step in achieving the goals that are critical to the sustainability challenges associated with growing demands for biofuels and edible oils.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Methods Summary"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Methods Summary</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequencing and assembly, genetic mapping and gene annotation</h3><p>Sequencing reads (Roche/454) were generated from genomic and BAC pool DNA fragment and paired-end linker libraries (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#MOESM16">Supplementary Figs 1–3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#MOESM16">Supplementary Tables 1–3</a>). BAC end sequencing was performed using the Sanger method. Sequence data were assembled as described (Methods). The genetic map for the selfed Nigerian <i>tenera</i> palm T128 was constructed as described (Methods). Gene predictions, genome and protein comparisons and gene ontology annotation were performed as described (Methods).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Transcriptome sequencing</h3><p>Thirty transcriptome libraries were constructed and transcriptome sequences were generated (Roche/454) (Methods). Transcriptome libraries from kernel and mesocarp were constructed and deep sequenced by Illumina HiSeq 2000 (Methods).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Methylation filtration</h3><p>Methylation filtration libraries were constructed as described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Palmer, L. E. et al. Maize genome sequencing by methylation filtration. Science 302, 2115–2117 (2003)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1556">15</a></sup> and resulting clones were sequenced using the Sanger method (Methods).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Phylogenetic analysis</h3><p>Open reading frames were assembled from evidence-based gene models and combined with a large seed plant data set<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Lee, E. K. et al. A functional phylogenomic view of the seed plants. PLoS Genet. 7, e1002411 (2011)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1568">26</a></sup>(Methods).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec7" data-title="Online Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec7-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec7">Online Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec7-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Genome assembly</h3><p>The assembly of <i>E. guineensis</i> (AVROS, <i>pisifera</i> fruit form) genome P5-build was constructed from sequences from a total of 148 linker libraries and 81 fragment libraries (Roche/454). Reads were generated from genomic DNA fragment libraries (53.5 million reads), BAC pool DNA fragment libraries (3.6 million reads), and from a series of genomic (89.1 million reads) and BAC (8.6 million reads) paired-end linker libraries. In total, 46.8 billion bases of raw sequence were generated, representing approximately 26-fold raw sequence coverage of the 1.8-Gb oil palm genome. Sequence data were assembled using the Newbler algorithm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Margulies, M. et al. Genome sequencing in microfabricated high-density picolitre reactors. Nature 437, 376–380 (2005)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1591">28</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#MOESM16">Supplementary Table 4</a>). The seventh assembly and genome build of <i>E. oleifera</i> (O7-build) was constructed from a total of 127 linker libraries and 68 fragment libraries. In total, 130 million <i>E. oleifera</i> reads, representing approximately 25-fold raw sequence coverage, were generated (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#MOESM16">Supplementary Table 2</a>).</p><p>High information content restriction fragment fingerprints were generated for 124,286 BAC clones from the <i>E. guineensis</i> genome with an average size of 150 kb. BAC fingerprints were used to construct a physical map of the reference genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#MOESM16">Supplementary Figs 2 and 3</a>). In addition, BAC ends from this library were sequenced using Sanger sequencing to generate 235,613 paired reads with an average read length of approximately 600 bp. BAC end reads were used to create the shotgun assembly, as well as to locate individual BACs within the assembled genome.</p><p>Before assembly, all Roche/454 sequence runs were screened for quality based on average read length, linker library efficiency and library redundancy using a custom pipeline based on the SSAHA program<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Ning, Z., Cox, A. J. &amp; Mullikin, J. C. SSAHA: a fast search method for large DNA databases. Genome Res. 11, 1725–1729 (2001)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1620">29</a></sup>. For linker positive reads, library insert sizes were estimated by aligning both ends to a draft assembly of oil palm, and measuring the intervening sequence in cases in which both ends matched a single draft scaffold. In order to minimize the negative impact of chimaeras in the linker libraries, we removed all identical reads that were due to library redundancy, not independent observations. In addition to the Roche/454 data, a set of 235,613 BAC end reads from the Origen_1 BAC library were included in the P5 assembly, with an average BAC size of 150 kb. These data were assembled using the Newbler algorithm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Margulies, M. et al. Genome sequencing in microfabricated high-density picolitre reactors. Nature 437, 376–380 (2005)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1624">28</a></sup> on a Dell PowerEdge R910 server with 512 Gb of RAM, and 32 cores running Ubuntu Linux 10.04. The P5 assembly took 15 days and 5 h to complete.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Genetic map construction</h3><p>Two genetic maps were constructed. The first mapping population was derived from the self-pollination of the Nigerian <i>tenera</i> palm T128. The mapping population and map-construction methodology are described elsewhere<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Singh, R. et al. The oil palm SHELL gene controls oil yield and encodes a homologue of SEEDSTICK. Nature &#xA;                    http://dx.doi.org/10.1038/nature12356&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (this issue)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1639">30</a></sup>. The second mapping population comprised 87 palms obtained from a cross between Ulu Remis Deli <i>dura</i> (ENL48) and Yangambi <i>pisifera</i> (ML161) grown at the FELDA Research Station at Jerantut, Malaysia. Linkage analysis for the <i>dura</i> × <i>pisifera</i> (P2) cross was performed using both JoinMap 4.0 and GenStat 14th edition. JoinMap was used to examine markers and identify loci showing distorted segregation (chi-squared test). JoinMap was initially used to construct the two parental framework maps at recombination frequency ≤ 0.2 and a nearest neighbour stress value of ≤ 4 (centimorgan) using the maximum likelihood mapping algorithm as described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Jansen, J. Construction of linkage maps in full-sib families of diploid outbreeding species by minimizing the number of recombinations in hidden inheritance vectors. Genetics 170, 2013–2025 (2005)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1656">31</a></sup>. The density of the linkage maps was later increased by mapping additional co-dominant markers into the parental framework maps using the maximum likelihood mapping algorithm in GenStat 14. The integrated map was built using the maximum likelihood mapping method in GenStat14 by combining data from markers on both the two parental maps. Comparison between the integrated map and the parental maps was visualized using MapChart 2.2 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Voorrips, R. E. MapChart: software for the graphical presentation of linkage maps and QTLs. J. Hered. 93, 77–78 (2002)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1659">32</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Genetic-map integration and chromosome-sequence construction</h3><p>The 1,511 markers used in the generation of the T128_codominant and P2_DxP maps were compared to scaffolds from the P5-build using the exonerate<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Slater, G. S. &amp; Birney, E. Automated generation of heuristics for biological sequence comparison. BMC Bioinformatics 6, 31 (2005)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1671">33</a></sup> program with an ungapped alignment and a minimum identity match of 97%. Markers that did not uniquely map to P5 scaffolds were discarded, and one scaffold marker ordering was created for each of the two maps. After reviewing shared scaffolds between the two maps, a final order was determined for ordering 169 scaffolds, and ordering and orienting 124 scaffolds based on multiple markers. The scaffold sequences were then concatenated in order and reverse complemented as required to create 16 linkage groups based chromosome sequences. During map integration, LG15 (T128_codominant naming convention) appeared significantly shorter than a previous integration based on the P4 assembly with the P2_regression map. This was owing to map instability introduced into the P2_DxP map generation in which the P2_regression was more stable. After review, the LG15 chromosome sequence was extended based on mapping of P5 scaffolds onto the P4/P2_regression version.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Gene identification and annotation</h3><p>Based on the longest set of scaffolds representing 10% of the original P1-build, we used the SNAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Korf, I. Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinformatics 5, 59 (2004)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1683">34</a></sup> gene finder to identify initial candidate gene predictions for the <i>E. guineensis</i> genome. Initial SNAP runs were performed using the rice (Os.hmm) gene model. Initial genes discovered were compared with the RefSeq35 database as well as the TIGR Gramineae repeat database in order to remove retroelements and pseudogenes. The remaining transcripts were then screened for missing start and stop codons, as well as other warnings from SNAP. The remaining set was then used as input to the SNAP programs FATHOM and FORGE according to the SNAP documentation to create a new hidden Markov model (HMM) with greater specificity to <i>E. guineensis.</i> The same screening process was applied again and four iterations of the training were used resulting in the final ‘pisif_2_22_11.hmm’ gene model.</p><p>For the Glimmer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Majoros, W. H., Pertea, M. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TigrScan and GlimmerHMM: two open source ab initio eukaryotic gene-finders. Bioinformatics 20, 2878–2879 (2004)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1696">35</a></sup> predictions, assembled <i>E. guineensis</i> transcriptome sequences (groups A, C, D and G in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#MOESM16">Supplementary Table 6</a>) were translated from start to stop with a size selection ranging from 500 to 5,000 nucleotides. These were then compared to complete coding sequences for Magnoliophyta from GenBank using BLASTX (<i>E</i>-value &lt; 10<sup>−10</sup>). Transcripts with homology starting at position one of the Magnoliophyta targets were selected for further analysis. The transcripts were then screened using BLASTClust (NCBI) and CD-HIT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Li, W. &amp; Godzik, A. Cd-hit: a fast program for clustering and comparing large sets of protein or nucleotide sequences. Bioinformatics 22, 1658–1659 (2006)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1712">36</a></sup> to reduce the number of genes to meet Glimmer’s training requirements. Exon boundaries were determined by mapping to the previous P4-build, and were used to create an <i>E. guineensis</i> HMM.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Transcriptome analysis</h3><p>Reads (Roche/454) from each library and from all libraries (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#Fig3">Fig. 3a, b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#MOESM16">Supplementary Table 6</a>) were assembled into isotigs respectively. The isotigs from each library were blasted on <i>A. thaliana</i> gene models with a threshold of <i>E</i>-value &lt; 10<sup>−5</sup>. The best hit <i>A. thaliana</i> gene model was assigned to the homologue of the query isotig. Based on Bourgis’ annotation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Bourgis, F. et al. Comparative transcriptome and metabolite analysis of oil palm and date palm mesocarp that differ dramatically in carbon partitioning. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 12527–12532 (2011)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1746">1</a></sup>, copy numbers were given to each gene in each category group. The final copy numbers of each functional group were scaled on the number of genes in each group. To estimate expression level of genes in mesocarp and kernel tissues, Illumina HiSeq 2000 reads from each library were mapped to assembled isotigs from all <i>E. guineensis</i> reads by using the Burrows–Wheeler Aligner. Gene group expression levels were calculated as the number of mapped reads on each isotig divided by the total number of isotigs, multiplied by 100,000, and scaled by the number of genes in each gene group. Both copy number and read coverage were the mean of measures from two biological replicates. Data were analysed as described above for Roche/454 data, except that expression levels were calculated as transcripts per million tags.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Methylation-filtered library analysis</h3><p>Methylation-filtered (‘GeneThresher’) genomic DNA libraries were constructed as described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Palmer, L. E. et al. Maize genome sequencing by methylation filtration. Science 302, 2115–2117 (2003)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1761">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Bedell, J. A. et al. Sorghum genome sequencing by methylation filtration. PLoS Biol. 3, e13 (2005)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1764">37</a></sup> to select unmethylated clones (depleted of most repetitive sequences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Ouyang, S. &amp; Buell, C. R. The TIGR Plant Repeat Databases: a collective resource for the identification of repetitive sequences in plants. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, D360–D363 (2004)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1768">38</a></sup>) by propagation in McrBC<sup>+</sup> strains of <i>Escherichia coli</i>. Briefly, nuclear DNA was extracted individually from young leaves of Deli <i>dura</i> and AVROS <i>pisifera</i> of <i>E. guineensis</i> and from <i>E. oleifera</i>. For each of the three DNA populations, genomic shotgun libraries were constructed as described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Bedell, J. A. et al. Sorghum genome sequencing by methylation filtration. PLoS Biol. 3, e13 (2005)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1790">37</a></sup>. Sequences were generated from one end of each cloned insert by ABI 3730 sequencing (Life Technologies), generating 298,039 reads (73,390 from Deli <i>dura</i>, 101,327 from AVROS <i>pisifera</i> and 123,322 from <i>E. oleifera</i>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Segmental duplication analysis</h3><p>Chromosomes from the EG5-linked assembly were screened in a self–self comparison test using the MUMmer3 set of tools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Kurtz, S. et al. Versatile and open software for comparing large genomes. Genome Biol. 5, R12 (2004)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1812">39</a></sup>. Final alignments were carried out on chromosome pairs using the PROmer program (optional parameters – d 0.5 –c 200), alignments were reviewed visually with the MUMmer plot program, and approximate boundaries for segmental duplications were recorded (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#MOESM16">Supplementary Fig. 7</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#MOESM16">Supplementary Table 7</a>).</p><p>To test for the existence of observed segmental duplications in other genomes, we performed comparative genomics of each half of the 16 segmental duplications in <i>E. guineensis</i> with the <i>E. oleifera</i> and <i>P. dactylifera</i> scaffold sets. Comparisons were performed using a custom analysis pipeline based on the MUMmer program (optional parameters –n –l 30 –b –c –L). MUMmer output was summarized as an offset-sorted overlap plot showing where query scaffolds share local alignment with a reference chromosome. Output was reviewed to verify that each half of the proposed segmental duplication was present in the query genome, and that the scaffolds matching were different for each half of the duplication in <i>E. guineensis</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Genome comparison by gene models</h3><p>NCBI TBLASTN program was used to compare <i>A. thaliana, O. sativa, P. dactylifera</i> and <i>E. guineensis</i> predicted proteins on the genomes of each of the four species, with a threshold of <i>E</i>-value &lt; 10<sup>−5</sup>. At this level of conservation, matches represent shared gene families between the query and target genomes. By comparing predicted proteins to genome sequence, biases introduced by gene prediction methods are minimized compared to a direct gene-level comparison. Comparisons between the gene models from one species and its own genome are close to, but less than 100%, owing to the limit of sensitivity of TBLASTN at this <i>E</i>-value cutoff. Public database sources for comparative genome sequences and gene models are provided in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature12309#MOESM16">Supplementary Methods</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Gene clustering and Venn diagram</h3><p>CD-HIT clustering algorithm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Li, W. &amp; Godzik, A. Cd-hit: a fast program for clustering and comparing large sets of protein or nucleotide sequences. Bioinformatics 22, 1658–1659 (2006)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1871">36</a></sup> was used to look for homologous protein sequences among <i>A. thaliana, O. sativa, P. dactylifera</i> and <i>E. guineensis</i> at 40% similarity level. This algorithm avoids all-versus-all BLAST search by using a short word filter.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Gene ontology annotation</h3><p><i>E. guineensis</i> gene model protein sequences were queried against <i>A. thaliana</i> annotated gene model protein sequences by using BLASTP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Altschul, S. F. et al. Gapped BLAST and PSI-BLAST: a new generation of protein database search programs. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 3389–3402 (1997)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1894">40</a></sup> with a threshold of <i>E</i>-value &lt; 10<sup>−5</sup>. The blast output file was then loaded into Blast2GO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Conesa, A. et al. Blast2GO: a universal tool for annotation, visualization and analysis in functional genomics research. Bioinformatics 21, 3674–3676 (2005)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1903">41</a></sup>. Blast2GO performed gene ontology annotation by using an annotation rule on the found ontology terms. The most specific annotations were assigned on each sequence with default parameters in the annotation rule.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Phylogenetic analysis</h3><p>A carefully annotated set of open reading frames was assembled from evidence-based gene models in each species. The oil palm open reading frames were combined with a large seed plant data set published previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Lee, E. K. et al. A functional phylogenomic view of the seed plants. PLoS Genet. 7, e1002411 (2011)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1916">26</a></sup>, with updated gene models for <i>Arabidopsis thaliana, Oryza sativa</i> and <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i>, as well as the addition of <i>P. dactylifera</i>, <i>M. acuminata</i>, <i>Carica papaya</i> and <i>Selaginella molendorffii</i> (outgroup) for a total of 107 taxa. Orthologues were identified using OrthologID<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Chiu, J. C. et al. OrthologID: automation of genome-scale ortholog identification within a parsimony framework. Bioinformatics 22, 699–707 (2006)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1939">42</a></sup>, and a sub-matrix with at least 30 taxa present per partition was extracted for phylogenetic analysis. This sub-matrix contains 1,685 gene partitions with 858,954 patterns. Maximum likelihood analysis was carried out using RAxML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML-VI-HPC: maximum likelihood-based phylogenetic analyses with thousands of taxa and mixed models. Bioinformatics 22, 2688–2690 (2006)" href="/articles/nature12309#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d21969e1943">43</a></sup> with the JTT+F+Γ model and 100 bootstrap replicates.</p></div></div></section>

R.S., M.O.-A., E.-T.L.L., R.N., N.L., J.M.O., S.W.S., R.K.W., R.S.F., R.A.M. and R.S. designed experiments and coordinated the project. R.S., M.O.A., E.-T.L.L., M.A.A.M., L.C.-L.O., S.-E.O., J.N., B.B., M.A.B., S.W.S., J.M.O. and R.S. conducted laboratory experiments and were involved in data analysis. E.-T.L.L., R.R., K.-L.C., M.A.H., N.A., S.W.S., D.H. and M.H. performed bioinformatics analyses. E.K.L. and R.D. carried out phylogenetic analysis. D.K., J.L.G. and R.A.W. built BAC libraries and carried out BAC end sequencing. R.S., M.O.A., E.-T.L.L., R.N., N.L., S.W.S., J.M.O., R.A.M. and R.S. participated in preparing and revising the manuscript. R.A.M. and R.S. supervised data generation and analysis.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding authors</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to

            <aside><div class="c-article-editorial-summary__container" data-component-show-more="true" id="editorial-summary"><section><h1 class="c-article-editorial-summary__title u-h3">Editorial Summary</h1><h2 class="c-article-editorial-summary__article-title">Oil palm genome reveals history of cultivation</h2><div class="c-article-editorial-summary__content c-article-editorial-summary__content--less"><p>Two papers published in this issue of <i>Nature</i> deal with the genetics of two variants of one of the most important crops in use today — the African oil palm <i>Elaeis guineensis</i> and its South American cousin <i>Elaeis oleifera</i>. Palm oil accounts for almost half the edible oil consumed worldwide and is also a biofuel, although not without controversy, as in many areas palm oil monoculture has replaced valuable natural forest. Analyses of the 1.8-gigabase genome sequence of <i>E. guineensis</i> and draft sequence of <i>E. oleifera</i> provide insights into oil biosynthesis genes and their regulators, and a record of genome evolution. A key event in the domestication and breeding of the oil palm was loss of the thick, coconut-like shell. The second of the two papers identifies mutations the <i>SHELL</i> gene that specify the different fruit forms found in the oil palm and shows that <i>SHELL</i> gene mutations that originated in pre-colonial Africa are responsible for the single gene hybrid vigour and high yields attained by the oil palm.</p></div></section></div></aside>
